</div></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Area<div class="ib-settlement-fn"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup </th><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1041539562">.mw-parser-output .citation{word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}</style></tr><tr><td colspan="2">
<span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_engfactobox"><b><a href="#ref_engfactobox">^</a></b></span> b. Area of Mexico City that includes non-urban areas at the south</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Mexico City</b> (Spanish: <i lang="es">Ciudad de México</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> <small>locally </small><span class="IPA" lang="es-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">[sjuˈða(ð) ðe ˈmexiko]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Es-Ciudad_de_Mexico.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/Es-Ciudad_de_Mexico.ogg" title="Es-Ciudad de Mexico.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> abbr.: CDMX; <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a>: <i>Altepetl Mexico</i>) is the <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primate_city" title="Primate city">largest city</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_American_cities_by_population" title="List of North American cities by population">most populous city</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>  One of the world's <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_World_Cities_Research_Network" title="Globalization and World Cities Research Network">alpha cities</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> it is located in the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Mexico" title="Valley of Mexico">Valley of Mexico</a> within the high Mexican central <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">plateau</a>, at an altitude of 2,240 meters (7,350 ft). The city has 16 <a href="/wiki/Boroughs_of_Mexico_City" title="Boroughs of Mexico City">boroughs or <i>demarcaciones territoriales</i></a>, which are in turn divided into <a href="/wiki/List_of_neighborhoods_in_Mexico_City" title="List of neighborhoods in Mexico City">neighborhoods or <i>colonias</i></a>.
</p><p>The 2020 population for the <a href="/wiki/City_proper" title="City proper">city proper</a> was 9,209,944,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegi_pop_2020_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-inegi_pop_2020-9">[9]</a></sup> with a land area of 1,495 square kilometers (577 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> According to the most recent definition agreed upon by the federal and state governments, the population of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Mexico_City" title="Greater Mexico City">Greater Mexico City</a> is 21,804,515, which makes it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_cities#List" title="List of largest cities">sixth-largest metropolitan area</a> in the world, the second-largest <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban agglomeration</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a> (behind <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>), and the largest <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish-speaking</a> city (city proper) in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greater_Mexico_City" title="Greater Mexico City">Greater Mexico City</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> of $411 billion in 2011, which makes it one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_by_GDP" title="List of cities by GDP">most productive urban areas in the world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> The city was responsible for generating 15.8% of Mexico's GDP, and the metropolitan area accounted for about 22% of the country's GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDP_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-GDP-21">[20]</a></sup> If it were an independent country in 2013, Mexico City would be the fifth-largest economy in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mexico's capital is both the <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_continuously_inhabited_cities#America,_Middle" title="List of oldest continuously inhabited cities">oldest capital city in the Americas</a> and one of two founded by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous people</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="what is the other city? (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The city was originally built on a group of islands in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Texcoco" title="Lake Texcoco">Lake Texcoco</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Mexica" title="Mexica">Mexica (Aztecs)</a> around 1325, under the name <a href="/wiki/Tenochtitlan" title="Tenochtitlan">Tenochtitlan</a>. It was almost completely destroyed in the 1521 <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Tenochtitlan" title="Fall of Tenochtitlan">Siege of Tenochtitlan</a> and subsequently redesigned and rebuilt in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_architecture#Spanish_Colonial_architecture" title="Spanish architecture">Spanish urban standards</a>. In 1524, the <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Mexico" title="Municipalities of Mexico">municipality</a> of Mexico City was established, known as <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">México Tenochtitlán</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gobdf_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-gobdf-23">[22]</a></sup> and as of 1585, it was officially known as <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Ciudad de México</i></span> (Mexico City).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gobdf_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-gobdf-23">[22]</a></sup> Mexico City was the political, administrative, and financial center of a major part of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish colonial empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-24">[23]</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/Mexican_War_of_Independence" title="Mexican War of Independence">independence from Spain</a> was achieved, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_district" title="Federal district">federal district</a> was created in 1824.
</p><p>After years of demanding greater political <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a>, residents were finally given the right to <a href="/wiki/Election" title="Election">elect</a> both a <a href="/wiki/List_of_mayors_of_Mexico_City" title="List of mayors of Mexico City">head of government</a> and the representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Unicameralism" title="Unicameralism">unicameral</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_Mexico_City" title="Legislative Assembly of Mexico City">Legislative Assembly</a> by election in 1997. Ever since, left-wing parties (first the <a href="/wiki/Party_of_the_Democratic_Revolution" title="Party of the Democratic Revolution">Party of the Democratic Revolution</a> and later the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Regeneration_Movement" title="National Regeneration Movement">National Regeneration Movement</a>) have controlled both of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> The city has several progressive policies, such as elective <a href="/wiki/Abortion_in_Mexico" title="Abortion in Mexico">abortions</a>, a limited form of <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia_in_Mexico" title="Euthanasia in Mexico">euthanasia</a>, <a href="/wiki/No-fault_divorce" title="No-fault divorce">no-fault divorce</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Mexico_City" title="Same-sex marriage in Mexico City">same-sex marriage</a>. On 29 January 2016, it ceased to be the <i>Federal District</i> (Spanish: <i lang="es">Distrito Federal</i> or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">D.F.</i></span>) and is now officially known as <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Ciudad de México</i></span> (or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">CDMX</i></span>), with a greater degree of autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Federal_District_Now_Officially_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Federal_District_Now_Officially-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> A clause in the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Mexico" title="Constitution of Mexico">Constitution of Mexico</a>, however, prevents it from becoming a state within the Mexican federation, as it is the seat of power in the country, unless the capital of the country were to be relocated elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN2-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicknames_and_mottos">Nicknames and mottos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nicknames and mottos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mexico City was traditionally known as <i>La Ciudad de los Palacios</i> ("the City of the Palaces"), a <a href="/wiki/Nickname" title="Nickname">nickname</a> attributed to Baron <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> when visiting the city in the 19th century, who, sending a letter back to Europe, said Mexico City could rival any major city in Europe. But it was English politician <a href="/wiki/Charles_La_Trobe" title="Charles La Trobe">Charles Latrobe</a> who really penned the following: "... look at their works: the moles, aqueducts, churches, roads—and the luxurious <i>City of Palaces</i> which has risen from the clay-builts ruins of Tenochtitlan...", on page 84 of the Letter V of <i>The Rambler in Mexico</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> During the colonial period, the city's <a href="/wiki/Motto" title="Motto">motto</a> was "Muy Noble e Insigne, Muy Leal e Imperial" (Very Noble and Distinguished, Very Loyal and Imperial).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Manuel_L%C3%B3pez_Obrador" title="Andrés Manuel López Obrador">Andrés López Obrador</a>'s administration a political slogan was introduced: <i>la Ciudad de la Esperanza</i> ("The City of Hope"). This motto was quickly adopted as a city nickname but has faded since the new motto, <i>Capital en Movimiento</i> ("Capital in Movement"), was adopted by the administration headed by <a href="/wiki/Marcelo_Ebrard" title="Marcelo Ebrard">Marcelo Ebrard</a>, though the latter is not treated as often as a nickname in media. Since 2013, to refer to the City, particularly in relation to government campaigns, the abbreviation CDMX has been used (from Ciudad de México),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> prior to this but recently,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2022)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> the abbreviation was "DF" (from Distrito Federal de México).
</p><p>The city is colloquially known as <i>Chilangolandia</i> after the locals' nickname <i><a href="/wiki/Chilango" title="Chilango">chilangos</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Chilango is used pejoratively by people living outside Mexico City to "connote a loud, arrogant, ill-mannered, loutish person".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> For their part those living in Mexico City designate insultingly those who live elsewhere as living in <i>la provincia</i> ("the provinces", the periphery) and many proudly embrace the term chilango.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> Residents of Mexico City are more recently called <i>defeños</i> (deriving from the postal abbreviation of the Federal District in Spanish: D.F., which is read "De-Efe"). They are formally called <i>capitalinos</i> (in reference to the city being the capital of the country), but "[p]erhaps because capitalino is the more polite, specific, and correct word, it is almost never utilized".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mexico_City" title="History of Mexico City">History of Mexico City</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a chronological guide, see <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Mexico_City" title="Timeline of Mexico City">Timeline of Mexico City</a>.</div>
<p>The oldest signs of human occupation in the area of Mexico City are those of the "<a href="/wiki/Pe%C3%B1on_woman" title="Peñon woman">Peñón woman</a>" and others found in San Bartolo Atepehuacan (<a href="/wiki/Gustavo_A._Madero,_Mexico_City" title="Gustavo A. Madero, Mexico City">Gustavo A. Madero</a>). They were believed to correspond to the lower Cenolithic period (9500–7000 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> However, a 2003 study placed the age of the Peñon woman at 12,700 years old (calendar age),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> one of the oldest human remains discovered in the Americas. Studies of her mitochondrial DNA suggest she was either of Asian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> or European<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> or Aboriginal Australian origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The area was the destination of the migrations of the <a href="/wiki/Chichimeca" title="Chichimeca">Teochichimecas</a> during the 8th and 13th centuries, people that would give rise to the <a href="/wiki/Toltec" title="Toltec">Toltec</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Mexica</a> (Aztecs) cultures. The latter arrived around the 14th century to settle first on the shores of the lake.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aztec_period">Aztec period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Aztec period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexico-Tenochtitlan" title="Mexico-Tenochtitlan">Mexico-Tenochtitlan</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_pre-columbian_archaeological_sites_in_Mexico_City" title="List of pre-columbian archaeological sites in Mexico City">List of pre-columbian archaeological sites in Mexico City</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Painting_of_Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco_on_Lake_Texcoco_(9755215791).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2491" data-file-width="4752" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Painting_of_Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco_on_Lake_Texcoco_%289755215791%29.jpg/220px-Painting_of_Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco_on_Lake_Texcoco_%289755215791%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Painting_of_Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco_on_Lake_Texcoco_%289755215791%29.jpg/330px-Painting_of_Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco_on_Lake_Texcoco_%289755215791%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Painting_of_Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco_on_Lake_Texcoco_%289755215791%29.jpg/440px-Painting_of_Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco_on_Lake_Texcoco_%289755215791%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Painting_of_Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco_on_Lake_Texcoco_(9755215791).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The city was the place of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexico-Tenochtitlan" title="Mexico-Tenochtitlan">Mexico-Tenochtitlan</a>, the Aztec capital.</div></div></div>
<p>The city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexico-Tenochtitlan" title="Mexico-Tenochtitlan">Mexico-Tenochtitlan</a> was founded by the <a href="/wiki/Mexica" title="Mexica">Mexica</a> people in 1325 or 1327.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> The old Mexica city that is now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Tenochtitlan" title="Tenochtitlan">Tenochtitlan</a> was built on an island in the center of the inland lake system of the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Mexico" title="Valley of Mexico">Valley of Mexico</a>, which is shared with a smaller city-state called <a href="/wiki/Tlatelolco_(altepetl)" title="Tlatelolco (altepetl)">Tlatelolco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> According to legend, the Mexicas' principal god, <a href="/wiki/Hu%C4%ABtzil%C5%8Dp%C5%8Dchtli" title="Huītzilōpōchtli">Huitzilopochtli</a>, indicated the site where they were to build their home by presenting a <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> perched on a <a href="/wiki/Opuntia" title="Opuntia">prickly pear</a> devouring a <a href="/wiki/Rattlesnake" title="Rattlesnake">rattlesnake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frances_F._Berdan_1982,_p._14_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frances_F._Berdan_1982,_p._14-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1325 and 1521, Tenochtitlan grew in size and strength, eventually dominating the other city-states around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Texcoco" title="Lake Texcoco">Lake Texcoco</a> and in the Valley of Mexico. When the Spaniards arrived, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> Empire had reached much of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, touching both the Gulf of Mexico and the Pacific Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frances_F._Berdan_1982,_p._14_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frances_F._Berdan_1982,_p._14-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_conquest">Spanish conquest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Spanish conquest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leutze,_Emanuel_%E2%80%94_Storming_of_the_Teocalli_by_Cortez_and_His_Troops_%E2%80%94_1848.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="921" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Leutze%2C_Emanuel_%E2%80%94_Storming_of_the_Teocalli_by_Cortez_and_His_Troops_%E2%80%94_1848.jpg/220px-Leutze%2C_Emanuel_%E2%80%94_Storming_of_the_Teocalli_by_Cortez_and_His_Troops_%E2%80%94_1848.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Leutze%2C_Emanuel_%E2%80%94_Storming_of_the_Teocalli_by_Cortez_and_His_Troops_%E2%80%94_1848.jpg/330px-Leutze%2C_Emanuel_%E2%80%94_Storming_of_the_Teocalli_by_Cortez_and_His_Troops_%E2%80%94_1848.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Leutze%2C_Emanuel_%E2%80%94_Storming_of_the_Teocalli_by_Cortez_and_His_Troops_%E2%80%94_1848.jpg/440px-Leutze%2C_Emanuel_%E2%80%94_Storming_of_the_Teocalli_by_Cortez_and_His_Troops_%E2%80%94_1848.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leutze,_Emanuel_%E2%80%94_Storming_of_the_Teocalli_by_Cortez_and_His_Troops_%E2%80%94_1848.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Storming of the Teocalli by Cortez and his Troops</i> (1848)</div></div></div>
<p>After landing in <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a>, Spanish explorer <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a> advanced upon Tenochtitlan with the aid of many of the other native peoples,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciudad_Mexico_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ciudad_Mexico-44">[43]</a></sup>
arriving there on 8 November 1519.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marroqui_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marroqui-45">[44]</a></sup> Cortés and his men marched along the causeway leading into the city from <a href="/wiki/Iztapalapa" title="Iztapalapa">Iztapalapa</a> (Ixtapalapa), and the city's ruler, <a href="/wiki/Moctezuma_II" title="Moctezuma II">Moctezuma II</a>, greeted the Spaniards; they exchanged gifts, but the camaraderie did not last long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs1_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs1-46">[45]</a></sup> Cortés put Moctezuma under <a href="/wiki/House_arrest" title="House arrest">house arrest</a>, hoping to rule through him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs2_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs2-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tensions increased until, on the night of 30 June 1520 – during a struggle known as "<a href="/wiki/La_Noche_Triste" title="La Noche Triste">La Noche Triste</a>" – the Aztecs rose up against the Spanish intrusion and managed to capture or drive out the Europeans and their <a href="/wiki/Tlaxcaltec" title="Tlaxcaltec">Tlaxcalan</a> allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs4_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs4-48">[47]</a></sup> Cortés regrouped at Tlaxcala. The Aztecs thought the Spaniards were permanently gone, and they elected a new king, <a href="/wiki/Cuitl%C3%A1huac" title="Cuitláhuac">Cuitláhuac</a>, but he soon died; the next king was <a href="/wiki/Cuauht%C3%A9moc" title="Cuauhtémoc">Cuauhtémoc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs6_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs6-49">[48]</a></sup> Cortés began a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tenochtitlan" title="Siege of Tenochtitlan">siege of Tenochtitlan</a> in May 1521. For three months, the city suffered from the lack of food and water as well as the spread of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> brought by the Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciudad_Mexico_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ciudad_Mexico-44">[43]</a></sup> Cortés and his allies landed their forces in the south of the island and slowly fought their way through the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs7_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs7-50">[49]</a></sup> Cuauhtémoc surrendered in August 1521.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciudad_Mexico_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ciudad_Mexico-44">[43]</a></sup> The Spaniards practically razed Tenochtitlan during the final siege of the conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marroqui_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marroqui-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cortés first settled in <a href="/wiki/Coyoac%C3%A1n" title="Coyoacán">Coyoacán</a>, but decided to rebuild the Aztec site to erase all traces of the old order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marroqui_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marroqui-45">[44]</a></sup> He did not establish a territory under his own <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">personal rule</a>, but remained loyal to the Spanish crown. The first Spanish <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">viceroy</a> arrived in Mexico City fourteen years later. By that time, the city had again become a <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a>, having power that extended far beyond its borders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc_Mex_2000_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enc_Mex_2000-51">[50]</a></sup> Although the Spanish preserved Tenochtitlan's basic layout, they built <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic churches</a> over the old Aztec temples and claimed the imperial palaces for themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc_Mex_2000_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Enc_Mex_2000-51">[50]</a></sup> Tenochtitlan was renamed "Mexico" because the Spanish found the word easier to pronounce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marroqui_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marroqui-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_colonial_Mexico_City">Growth of colonial Mexico City</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Growth of colonial Mexico City">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_colonial_churches_in_Mexico_City" title="List of colonial churches in Mexico City">List of colonial churches in Mexico City</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Colonial_government_and_civil_buildings_in_Mexico_City" title="List of Colonial government and civil buildings in Mexico City">List of Colonial government and civil buildings in Mexico City</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ignacio_Serrano_-_Catedral_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="711" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ignacio_Serrano_-_Catedral_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg/220px-Ignacio_Serrano_-_Catedral_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ignacio_Serrano_-_Catedral_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg/330px-Ignacio_Serrano_-_Catedral_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ignacio_Serrano_-_Catedral_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg/440px-Ignacio_Serrano_-_Catedral_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ignacio_Serrano_-_Catedral_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mexico_City_Metropolitan_Cathedral" title="Mexico City Metropolitan Cathedral">Mexico City Metropolitan Cathedral</a>'s (1571–1813) 18th century painting. The cathedral was built by the Spaniards over the ruins of the main Aztec temple.</div></div></div>
<p>The city had been the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Aztec_Empire" title="Aztec Empire">Aztec Empire</a> and in the colonial era, Mexico City became the capital of <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroy_of_Mexico" title="Viceroy of Mexico">viceroy of Mexico</a> or vice-king lived in the viceregal palace on the main square or <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%B3calo" title="Zócalo">Zócalo</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City_Metropolitan_Cathedral" title="Mexico City Metropolitan Cathedral">Mexico City Metropolitan Cathedral</a>, the seat of the Archbishopric of New Spain, was constructed on another side of the Zócalo, as was the archbishop's palace, and across from it the building housing the city council or <i>ayuntamiento</i> of the city. A late seventeenth-century painting of the Zócalo by <a href="/wiki/Crist%C3%B3bal_de_Villalpando" title="Cristóbal de Villalpando">Cristóbal de Villalpando</a> depicts the main square, which had been the old Aztec ceremonial center. The existing central place of the Aztecs was effectively and permanently transformed to the ceremonial center and seat of power during the colonial period, and remains to this day in modern Mexico, the central place of the nation. The rebuilding of the city after the siege of Tenochtitlan was accomplished by the abundant indigenous labor in the surrounding area. Franciscan friar <a href="/wiki/Toribio_de_Benavente_Motolinia" title="Toribio de Benavente Motolinia">Toribio de Benavente Motolinia</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles_of_Mexico" title="Twelve Apostles of Mexico">Twelve Apostles of Mexico</a> who arrived in New Spain in 1524, described the rebuilding of the city as one of the afflictions or plagues of the early period:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The seventh plague was the construction of the great City of Mexico, which, during the early years used more people than in the construction of Jerusalem. The crowds of laborers were so numerous that one could hardly move in the streets and causeways, although they are very wide. Many died from being crushed by beams, or falling from high places, or in tearing down old buildings for new ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forma_y_Levantado_de_La_Ciudad_de_Mexico,_1628,_por_Juan_Gomez_de_Trasmonte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="11612" data-file-width="14592" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Forma_y_Levantado_de_La_Ciudad_de_Mexico%2C_1628%2C_por_Juan_Gomez_de_Trasmonte.jpg/220px-Forma_y_Levantado_de_La_Ciudad_de_Mexico%2C_1628%2C_por_Juan_Gomez_de_Trasmonte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Forma_y_Levantado_de_La_Ciudad_de_Mexico%2C_1628%2C_por_Juan_Gomez_de_Trasmonte.jpg/330px-Forma_y_Levantado_de_La_Ciudad_de_Mexico%2C_1628%2C_por_Juan_Gomez_de_Trasmonte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Forma_y_Levantado_de_La_Ciudad_de_Mexico%2C_1628%2C_por_Juan_Gomez_de_Trasmonte.jpg/440px-Forma_y_Levantado_de_La_Ciudad_de_Mexico%2C_1628%2C_por_Juan_Gomez_de_Trasmonte.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forma_y_Levantado_de_La_Ciudad_de_Mexico,_1628,_por_Juan_Gomez_de_Trasmonte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mexico City in 1628</div></div></div>
<p>Preconquest Tenochtitlan was built in the center of the inland lake system, with the city reachable by <a href="/wiki/Canoe" title="Canoe">canoe</a> and by wide causeways to the mainland. The causeways were rebuilt under Spanish rule with indigenous labor. Colonial Spanish cities were constructed on a grid pattern, if no geographical obstacle prevented it. In Mexico City, the Zócalo (main square) was the central place from which the grid was then built outward. The Spanish lived in the area closest to the main square in what was known as the <i>traza</i>, in orderly, well laid-out streets. Indigenous residences were outside that exclusive zone and houses were haphazardly located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Spaniards sought to keep indigenous people separate but since the Zócalo was a center of commerce for Amerindians, they were a constant presence in the central area, so strict segregation was never enforced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> At intervals Zócalo was where major celebrations took place as well as executions. It was also the site of two major riots in the seventeenth century, one in 1624, the other in 1692.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The city grew as the population did, coming up against the lake's waters. As the depth of the lake water fluctuated, Mexico City was subject to periodic flooding. A major labor draft, the <i>desagüe</i>, compelled thousands of indigenous over the colonial period to work on infrastructure to prevent flooding. Floods were not only an inconvenience but also a health hazard, since during flood periods human waste polluted the city's streets. By draining the area, the mosquito population dropped as did the frequency of the diseases they spread. However, draining the wetlands also changed the habitat for fish and birds and the areas accessible for indigenous cultivation close to the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> The 16th century saw a proliferation of churches, many of which can still be seen today in the <a href="/wiki/Historic_center_of_Mexico_City" title="Historic center of Mexico City">historic center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc_Mex_2000_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Enc_Mex_2000-51">[50]</a></sup>
Economically, Mexico City prospered as a result of trade. Unlike Brazil or <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, Mexico had easy contact with both the Atlantic and Pacific worlds. Although the Spanish crown tried to completely regulate all commerce in the city, it had only partial success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamnett_1998_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamnett_1998-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palacio_de_mineria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1272" data-file-width="1615" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Palacio_de_mineria.jpg/220px-Palacio_de_mineria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Palacio_de_mineria.jpg/330px-Palacio_de_mineria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Palacio_de_mineria.jpg/440px-Palacio_de_mineria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palacio_de_mineria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Palacio de Mineria, Mexico City. The elevation of silver mining as a profession and the ennoblement of silver miners was a development of the eighteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Reforms" title="Bourbon Reforms">Bourbon Reforms</a></div></div></div>
<p>The concept of <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> flourished in New Spain in a way not seen in other parts of the Americas. Spaniards encountered a society in which the concept of nobility mirrored that of their own. Spaniards respected the indigenous order of nobility and added to it. In the ensuing centuries, possession of a <a href="/wiki/Mexican_nobility" title="Mexican nobility">noble title in Mexico</a> did not mean one exercised great political power, for one's power was limited even if the accumulation of wealth was not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ladd_1998_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ladd_1998-58">[57]</a></sup> The concept of nobility in Mexico was not political but rather a very conservative Spanish social one, based on proving the worthiness of the family. Most of these families proved their worth by making fortunes in New Spain outside of the city itself, then spending the revenues in the capital, building churches, supporting charities and building extravagant palatial homes. The craze to build the most opulent residence possible reached its height in the last half of the 18th century. Many of these palaces can still be seen today, leading to Mexico City's nickname of "The city of palaces" given by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander Von Humboldt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marroqui_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marroqui-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc_Mex_2000_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Enc_Mex_2000-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ladd_1998_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ladd_1998-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grito_de_Dolores" title="Grito de Dolores">Grito de Dolores</a> ("Cry of Dolores"), also known as El Grito de la Independencia ("Cry of Independence"), marked the beginning of the Mexican War of Independence. The Battle of Guanajuato, the first major engagement of the insurgency, occurred four days later. After a decade of war, Mexico's independence from Spain was effectively declared in the Declaration of Independence of the Mexican Empire on 27 September 1821.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iturbide_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iturbide-59">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Agust%C3%ADn_de_Iturbide" title="Agustín de Iturbide">Agustín de Iturbide</a> is proclaimed Emperor of the <a href="/wiki/First_Mexican_Empire" title="First Mexican Empire">First Mexican Empire</a> by Congress, crowned in the <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City_Metropolitan_Cathedral" title="Mexico City Metropolitan Cathedral">Cathedral of Mexico</a>. Unrest followed for the next several decades, as different factions fought for control of Mexico.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_Federal_District" title="Mexican Federal District">Mexican Federal District</a> was established by the new government and by the signing of their new constitution, where the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Federal_district" title="Federal district">federal district</a> was adapted from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Countries_of_the_World_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Countries_of_the_World-60">[59]</a></sup> Before this designation, Mexico City had served as the <a href="/wiki/Seat_of_government" title="Seat of government">seat of government</a> for both the <a href="/wiki/State_of_Mexico" title="State of Mexico">State of Mexico</a> and the nation as a whole. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Texcoco_de_Mora,_State_of_Mexico" title="Texcoco de Mora, State of Mexico">Texcoco de Mora</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Toluca" title="Toluca">Toluca</a> became the capital of the State of Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Battle_of_Mexico_City_in_the_U.S..E2.80.93Mexican_War_of_1847"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Mexico_City_in_the_U.S.–Mexican_War_of_1847">Battle of Mexico City in the U.S.–Mexican War of 1847</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Battle of Mexico City in the U.S.–Mexican War of 1847">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Chapultepec.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Battle_of_Chapultepec.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Chapultepec.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Battle_of_Chapultepec.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Chapultepec.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Battle_of_Chapultepec.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Chapultepec.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Chapultepec.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>A painting of the American assault on the <a href="/wiki/Chapultepec_Castle" title="Chapultepec Castle">Chapultepec Castle</a>.</i></div></div></div>
<p>During the 19th century, Mexico City was the center stage of all the political disputes of the country. It was the imperial capital on two occasions (1821–1823 and 1864–1867), and of two <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalist</a> states and two <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">centralist</a> states that followed innumerable coups d'états in the space of half a century before the triumph of the Liberals after the <a href="/wiki/Reform_War" title="Reform War">Reform War</a>. It was also the objective of one of the two French invasions to Mexico (<a href="/wiki/Second_French_intervention_in_Mexico" title="Second French intervention in Mexico">1861–1867</a>), and occupied for a year by American troops in the framework of the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a> (1847–1848).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_for_Mexico_City" title="Battle for Mexico City">Battle for Mexico City</a> was the series of engagements from 8 to 15 September 1847, in the general vicinity of Mexico City during the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">U.S. Mexican War</a>. Included are major actions at the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Molino_del_Rey" title="Battle of Molino del Rey">Molino del Rey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Chapultepec</a>, culminating with the fall of Mexico City. The U.S. Army under Winfield Scott scored a major success that ended the war. The American invasion into the Federal District was first resisted during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Churubusco" title="Battle of Churubusco">Battle of Churubusco</a> on 8 August, where the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Battalion" title="Saint Patrick's Battalion">Saint Patrick's Battalion</a>, which was composed primarily of Catholic Irish and German immigrants but also Canadians, English, French, Italians, Poles, Scots, Spaniards, Swiss, and Mexicans, fought for the Mexican cause, repelling the American attacks. After defeating the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Battalion" title="Saint Patrick's Battalion">Saint Patrick's Battalion</a>, the Mexican–American War came to a close after the United States <a href="/wiki/Military_deployment" title="Military deployment">deployed</a> <a href="/wiki/Military_organization" title="Military organization">combat units</a> deep into <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> resulting in the capture of Mexico City and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veracruz,_Veracruz" title="Veracruz, Veracruz">Veracruz</a> by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army's</a> 1st, 2nd, 3rd and 4th <a href="/wiki/Division_(military)" title="Division (military)">Divisions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cerro_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cerro-62">[61]</a></sup> The invasion culminated with the storming of <a href="/wiki/Chapultepec_Castle" title="Chapultepec Castle">Chapultepec Castle</a> in the city itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Storming_of_Chapultepec_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Storming_of_Chapultepec-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>During this battle, on 13 September, the 4th Division, under <a href="/wiki/John_A._Quitman" title="John A. Quitman">John A. Quitman</a>, spearheaded the attack against Chapultepec and carried the castle. Future <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> generals <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_E._Pickett" title="George E. Pickett">George E. Pickett</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Longstreet" title="James Longstreet">James Longstreet</a> participated in the attack. Serving in the Mexican defense were the cadets later immortalized as <i><a href="/wiki/Ni%C3%B1os_H%C3%A9roes" title="Niños Héroes">Los Niños Héroes</a></i> (the "Boy Heroes"). The Mexican forces fell back from Chapultepec and retreated within the city. Attacks on the Belén and San Cosme Gates came afterwards. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a> was signed in what is now the far north of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-treaty_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-treaty-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Porfirian_era_.281876.E2.80.931911.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Porfirian_era_(1876–1911)">Porfirian era (1876–1911)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Porfirian era (1876–1911)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Porforio_Diaz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="871" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Porforio_Diaz.jpg/220px-Porforio_Diaz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Porforio_Diaz.jpg/330px-Porforio_Diaz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Porforio_Diaz.jpg/440px-Porforio_Diaz.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Porforio_Diaz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mexican President and later dictator <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">Porfirio Díaz</a> (second from right) commissioned many of the ornate European style buildings constructed from the 1890–1910 and hoped for Mexico City to eventually rival European cities like Paris in opulence</div></div></div>
<p>Events such as the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Intervention" title="French Intervention">French Intervention</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Reform_War" title="Reform War">Reform War</a> left the city relatively untouched and it continued to grow, especially during the rule of President <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">Porfirio Díaz</a>. During this time the city developed a modern infrastructure, such as roads, schools, transportation systems and communication systems. However the regime concentrated resources and wealth into the city while the rest of the country languished in poverty.
</p><p>Under the rule of Porfirio Díaz, Mexico City experienced a massive transformation. Díaz's goal was to create a city which could rival the great European cities. He and his government came to the conclusion that they would use Paris as a model, while still containing remnants of Amerindian and Hispanic elements. This style of Mexican-French fusion architecture became colloquially known as Porfirian Architecture. Porfirian architecture became very influenced by Paris' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haussmannization" title="Haussmannization">Haussmannization</a>.
</p><p>During this era of Porfirian rule, the city underwent an extensive modernization. Many Spanish Colonial style buildings were destroyed, replaced by new much larger Porfirian institutions and many outlying rural zones were transformed into urban or industrialized districts with most having electrical, gas and sewage utilities by 1908. While the initial focus was on developing modern hospitals, schools, factories and massive public works, perhaps the most long-lasting effects of the Porfirian modernization were creation of the Colonia Roma area and the development of Reforma Avenue. Many of Mexico City's major attractions and landmarks were built during this era in this style.
</p><p>Diaz's plans called for the entire city to eventually be modernized or rebuilt in the Porfirian/French style of the Colonia Roma; but the Mexican Revolution began soon afterward and the plans never came to fruition, with many projects being left half-completed. One of the best examples of this is the Monument to the Mexican Revolution. Originally the monument was to be the main dome of Diaz's new senate hall, but when the revolution erupted only the dome of the senate hall and its supporting pillars were completed, this was subsequently seen as a symbol by many Mexicans that the Porfirian era was over once and for all and as such, it was turned into a monument to victory over Diaz.
</p>
<h3><span id="Mexican_Revolution_.281910.E2.80.931920.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexican_Revolution_(1910–1920)">Mexican Revolution (1910–1920)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mexican Revolution (1910–1920)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dead_outside_National_Palace_during_one_of_the_outbreaks,_Mexico_City.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="1450" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Dead_outside_National_Palace_during_one_of_the_outbreaks%2C_Mexico_City.jpg/220px-Dead_outside_National_Palace_during_one_of_the_outbreaks%2C_Mexico_City.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Dead_outside_National_Palace_during_one_of_the_outbreaks%2C_Mexico_City.jpg/330px-Dead_outside_National_Palace_during_one_of_the_outbreaks%2C_Mexico_City.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Dead_outside_National_Palace_during_one_of_the_outbreaks%2C_Mexico_City.jpg/440px-Dead_outside_National_Palace_during_one_of_the_outbreaks%2C_Mexico_City.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dead_outside_National_Palace_during_one_of_the_outbreaks,_Mexico_City.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Corpses in front of the National Palace during the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Tragic_Days" title="Ten Tragic Days">Ten Tragic Days</a>. Photographer, Manuel Ramos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The capital escaped the worst of the violence of the ten-year conflict of the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a>. The most significant episode of this period for the city was the February 1913 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decena_tr%C3%A1gica" title="Decena trágica">la Decena Trágica</a> ("The Ten Tragic Days"), when forces counter to the elected government of <a href="/wiki/Francisco_I._Madero" title="Francisco I. Madero">Francisco I. Madero</a> staged a successful coup. The center of the city was subjected to artillery attacks from the army stronghold of the <i>ciudadela</i> or citadel, with significant civilian casualties and the undermining of confidence in the Madero government. <a href="/wiki/Victoriano_Huerta" title="Victoriano Huerta">Victoriano Huerta</a>, chief general of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huerta%27s_Federal_Army" title="Huerta's Federal Army">Federal Army</a>, saw a chance to take power, forcing Madero and Pino Suarez to sign resignations. The two were murdered later while on their way to <a href="/wiki/Palacio_de_Lecumberri" title="Palacio de Lecumberri">Lecumberri prison</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redescolar_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-redescolar-66">[65]</a></sup> Huerta's ouster in July 1914 saw the entry of the armies of <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Villa" title="Pancho Villa">Pancho Villa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emiliano_Zapata" title="Emiliano Zapata">Emiliano Zapata</a>, but the city did not experience violence. Huerta had abandoned the capital and the conquering armies marched in. <a href="/wiki/Venustiano_Carranza" title="Venustiano Carranza">Venustiano Carranza</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutionalist" title="Constitutionalist">Constitutionalist</a> faction ultimately prevailed in the revolutionary civil war and Carranza took up residence in the presidential palace.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century_to_present">20th century to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 20th century to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Casa_Estudio_Diego_Rivera_y_Frida_Kahlo_(2566206309).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Casa_Estudio_Diego_Rivera_y_Frida_Kahlo_%282566206309%29.jpg/220px-Casa_Estudio_Diego_Rivera_y_Frida_Kahlo_%282566206309%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Casa_Estudio_Diego_Rivera_y_Frida_Kahlo_%282566206309%29.jpg/330px-Casa_Estudio_Diego_Rivera_y_Frida_Kahlo_%282566206309%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Casa_Estudio_Diego_Rivera_y_Frida_Kahlo_%282566206309%29.jpg/440px-Casa_Estudio_Diego_Rivera_y_Frida_Kahlo_%282566206309%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Casa_Estudio_Diego_Rivera_y_Frida_Kahlo_(2566206309).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Frida_Kahlo" title="Frida Kahlo">Frida Kahlo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diego_Rivera" title="Diego Rivera">Diego Rivera</a> house in <a href="/wiki/San_%C3%81ngel" title="San Ángel">San Ángel</a> designed by <a href="/wiki/Juan_O%27Gorman" title="Juan O'Gorman">Juan O'Gorman</a>, an example of 20th-century Modernist architecture in Mexico</div></div></div>
<p>The history of the rest of the 20th century to the present focuses on the phenomenal growth of the city and its environmental and political consequences. In 1900, the population of Mexico City was about 500,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaRosa_2005_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaRosa_2005-67">[66]</a></sup> The city began to grow rapidly westward in the early part of the 20th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc_Mex_2000_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-Enc_Mex_2000-51">[50]</a></sup> and then began to grow upwards in the 1950s, with the <a href="/wiki/Torre_Latinoamericana" title="Torre Latinoamericana">Torre Latinoamericana</a> becoming the city's first skyscraper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciudad_Mexico_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ciudad_Mexico-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rapid development of Mexico City as a center for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist_architecture" title="Modernist architecture">modernist architecture</a> was most fully manifested in the mid-1950s construction of the <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_Universitaria,_Mexico_City" title="Ciudad Universitaria, Mexico City">Ciudad Universitaria, Mexico City</a>, the main campus of the <a href="/wiki/National_Autonomous_University_of_Mexico" title="National Autonomous University of Mexico">National Autonomous University of Mexico</a>. Designed by the most prestigious architects of the era, including <a href="/wiki/Mario_Pani" title="Mario Pani">Mario Pani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eugenio_Peschard" title="Eugenio Peschard">Eugenio Peschard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Enrique_del_Moral" title="Enrique del Moral">Enrique del Moral</a>, the buildings feature murals by artists <a href="/wiki/Diego_Rivera" title="Diego Rivera">Diego Rivera</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Alfaro_Siqueiros" title="David Alfaro Siqueiros">David Alfaro Siqueiros</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Ch%C3%A1vez_Morado" title="José Chávez Morado">José Chávez Morado</a>. It has since been recognized as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1968_Summer_Olympics" title="1968 Summer Olympics">1968 Olympic Games</a> brought about the construction of large sporting facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc_Mex_2000_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-Enc_Mex_2000-51">[50]</a></sup> In 1969, the <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City_Metro" title="Mexico City Metro">Metro system</a> was inaugurated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciudad_Mexico_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ciudad_Mexico-44">[43]</a></sup>
Explosive growth in the population of the city started in the 1960s, with the population overflowing the boundaries of the Federal District into the neighboring State of Mexico, especially to the north, northwest, and northeast. Between 1960 and 1980 the city's population more than doubled to nearly 9 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc_Mex_2000_51-6"><a href="#cite_note-Enc_Mex_2000-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1980 half of all the industrial jobs in Mexico were located in Mexico City. Under relentless growth, the Mexico City government could barely keep up with services. Villagers from the countryside who continued to pour into the city to escape poverty only compounded the city's problems. With no housing available, they took over lands surrounding the city, creating huge <a href="/wiki/Shanty_town" title="Shanty town">shantytowns</a> that extended for many miles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This caused serious <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution_in_Mexico_City" title="Air pollution in Mexico City">air pollution in Mexico City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a> problems, as well as <a href="/wiki/Groundwater-related_subsidence" title="Groundwater-related subsidence">subsidence due to overextraction of groundwater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-water_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-water-69">[68]</a></sup> Air and water pollution has been contained and improved in several areas due to government programs, the renovation of vehicles and the modernization of public transportation.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Estudiantes_sobre_cammi%C3%B3n_quemado_(A68).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2772" data-file-width="4512" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Estudiantes_sobre_cammi%C3%B3n_quemado_%28A68%29.JPG/220px-Estudiantes_sobre_cammi%C3%B3n_quemado_%28A68%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Estudiantes_sobre_cammi%C3%B3n_quemado_%28A68%29.JPG/330px-Estudiantes_sobre_cammi%C3%B3n_quemado_%28A68%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Estudiantes_sobre_cammi%C3%B3n_quemado_%28A68%29.JPG/440px-Estudiantes_sobre_cammi%C3%B3n_quemado_%28A68%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Estudiantes_sobre_cammi%C3%B3n_quemado_(A68).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Students in a burned bus during the <a href="/wiki/Tlatelolco_massacre" title="Tlatelolco massacre">protests of 1968</a></div></div></div>
<p>The autocratic government that ruled Mexico City since the Revolution was tolerated, mostly because of the continued economic expansion since World War II. This was the case even though this government could not handle the population and pollution problems adequately. Nevertheless, discontent and protests began in the 1960s leading to the <a href="/wiki/Tlatelolco_massacre" title="Tlatelolco massacre">massacre of an unknown number of protesting students</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tlatelolco_(Mexico_City)" title="Tlatelolco (Mexico City)">Tlatelolco</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Three years later, a demonstration in the Maestros avenue, organized by former members of the 1968 student movement, was violently repressed by a paramilitary group called "Los Halcones", composed of gang members and teenagers from many sports clubs who received training in the U.S.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paloma_Cordero_Nancy_Reagan_Mexico_City_1985_earthquake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Paloma_Cordero_Nancy_Reagan_Mexico_City_1985_earthquake.jpg/220px-Paloma_Cordero_Nancy_Reagan_Mexico_City_1985_earthquake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Paloma_Cordero_Nancy_Reagan_Mexico_City_1985_earthquake.jpg/330px-Paloma_Cordero_Nancy_Reagan_Mexico_City_1985_earthquake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Paloma_Cordero_Nancy_Reagan_Mexico_City_1985_earthquake.jpg/440px-Paloma_Cordero_Nancy_Reagan_Mexico_City_1985_earthquake.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paloma_Cordero_Nancy_Reagan_Mexico_City_1985_earthquake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First ladies <a href="/wiki/Paloma_Cordero" title="Paloma Cordero">Paloma Cordero</a> of Mexico (left) and <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Reagan" title="Nancy Reagan">Nancy Reagan</a> of the United States (right) with U.S. Ambassador to Mexico, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Gavin" title="John Gavin">John Gavin</a> observing the damage done by the <a href="/wiki/1985_Mexico_City_earthquake" title="1985 Mexico City earthquake">1985 earthquake</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>On Thursday, 19 September 1985, at 7:19 am <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Standard_Time" title="Central Standard Time">CST</a>, Mexico City was struck by an <a href="/wiki/1985_Mexico_City_earthquake" title="1985 Mexico City earthquake">earthquake of magnitude 8.1</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campus_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campus-70">[69]</a></sup> on the <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">Richter magnitude scale</a>. Although this earthquake was not as deadly or destructive as many similar events in Asia and other parts of Latin America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moreno_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moreno-71">[70]</a></sup> it proved to be a disaster politically for the <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party</a> government. The government was paralyzed by its own bureaucracy and corruption, forcing ordinary citizens to create and direct their own rescue efforts and to reconstruct much of the housing that was lost as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haber_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-haber-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the last straw may have been the controversial elections of 1988. That year, the presidency was set between the P.R.I.'s candidate, Carlos Salinas de Gortari, and a coalition of left-wing parties led by <a href="/wiki/Cuauht%C3%A9moc_C%C3%A1rdenas" title="Cuauhtémoc Cárdenas">Cuauhtémoc Cárdenas</a>, son of the former president <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1zaro_C%C3%A1rdenas" title="Lázaro Cárdenas">Lázaro Cárdenas</a>. The counting system "fell" because coincidentally the power went out and suddenly, when it returned, the winning candidate was Salinas, even though Cárdenas had the upper hand.
</p><p>As a result of the fraudulent election, Cárdenas became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Party_of_the_Democratic_Revolution" title="Party of the Democratic Revolution">Party of the Democratic Revolution</a>. Discontent over the election eventually led <a href="/wiki/Cuauht%C3%A9moc_C%C3%A1rdenas" title="Cuauhtémoc Cárdenas">Cuauhtémoc Cárdenas</a> to become the first <a href="/wiki/Mayor" title="Mayor">elected mayor</a> of Mexico City in 1997. Cárdenas promised a more <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic government</a>, and his party claimed some victories against crime, pollution, and other major problems. He resigned in 1999 to run for the presidency.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mexico_city_ESA348919.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1787" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Mexico_city_ESA348919.jpg/220px-Mexico_city_ESA348919.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Mexico_city_ESA348919.jpg/330px-Mexico_city_ESA348919.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Mexico_city_ESA348919.jpg/440px-Mexico_city_ESA348919.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mexico_city_ESA348919.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellite_image" title="Satellite image">Satellite image</a> of Mexico City</div></div></div>
<p>Mexico City is located in the Valley of Mexico, sometimes called the Basin of Mexico. This valley is located in the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Mexican_Volcanic_Belt" title="Trans-Mexican Volcanic Belt">Trans-Mexican Volcanic Belt</a> in the high plateaus of south-central Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porrua_Cuenca_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Porrua_Cuenca-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-case_study_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-case_study-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has a minimum altitude of 2,200 meters (7,200 feet) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a> and is surrounded by mountains and volcanoes that reach elevations of over 5,000 meters (16,000 feet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Research_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Research-75">[74]</a></sup> This valley has no natural drainage outlet for the waters that flow from the mountainsides, making the city vulnerable to flooding. Drainage was engineered through the use of canals and tunnels starting in the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porrua_Cuenca_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Porrua_Cuenca-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Research_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-National_Research-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mexico City primarily rests on what was <a href="/wiki/Lake_Texcoco" title="Lake Texcoco">Lake Texcoco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porrua_Cuenca_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Porrua_Cuenca-73">[72]</a></sup> Seismic activity is frequent there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yip_2002_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yip_2002-76">[75]</a></sup> Lake Texcoco was drained starting from the 17th century. Although none of the lake waters remain, the city rests on the lake bed's heavily saturated clay. This soft base is collapsing due to the over-extraction of groundwater, called <i><a href="/wiki/Groundwater-related_subsidence" title="Groundwater-related subsidence">groundwater-related subsidence</a></i>.
</p><p>Since the beginning of the 20th century the city has sunk as much as nine meters (30 feet) in some areas. On average Mexico City sinks 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inches" title="Inches">inches</a> (1 <a href="/wiki/Foot_(unit)" title="Foot (unit)">foot</a> and 8 inches) or 50 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimetres" title="Centimetres">centimetres</a> (1/2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meters" title="Meters">meters</a>) every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> This sinking is causing problems with runoff and wastewater management, leading to flooding problems, especially during the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Research_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-National_Research-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yip_2002_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yip_2002-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> The entire lake bed is now paved over and most of the city's remaining forested areas lie in the southern boroughs of <a href="/wiki/Milpa_Alta" title="Milpa Alta">Milpa Alta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tlalpan" title="Tlalpan">Tlalpan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xochimilco" title="Xochimilco">Xochimilco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yip_2002_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yip_2002-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="2" class="toc" style="margin:1em auto; width:600px; float:center; padding:0.5e text-align:left;clear:all; margin-left:3px; font-size:90%;">
